<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Evidence_of_human-induced_global_warming_-_patterns_of_temperature_change_produced_by_various_atmospheric_factors,_1958-1999_(USGCRP).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Evidence_of_human-induced_global_warming_-_patterns_of_temperature_change_produced_by_various_atmospheric_factors%2C_1958-1999_%28USGCRP%29.png/220px-Evidence_of_human-induced_global_warming_-_patterns_of_temperature_change_produced_by_various_atmospheric_factors%2C_1958-1999_%28USGCRP%29.png" width="220" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Evidence_of_human-induced_global_warming_-_patterns_of_temperature_change_produced_by_various_atmospheric_factors%2C_1958-1999_%28USGCRP%29.png/330px-Evidence_of_human-induced_global_warming_-_patterns_of_temperature_change_produced_by_various_atmospheric_factors%2C_1958-1999_%28USGCRP%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Evidence_of_human-induced_global_warming_-_patterns_of_temperature_change_produced_by_various_atmospheric_factors%2C_1958-1999_%28USGCRP%29.png/440px-Evidence_of_human-induced_global_warming_-_patterns_of_temperature_change_produced_by_various_atmospheric_factors%2C_1958-1999_%28USGCRP%29.png 2x" data-file-width="907" data-file-height="997" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Evidence_of_human-induced_global_warming_-_patterns_of_temperature_change_produced_by_various_atmospheric_factors,_1958-1999_(USGCRP).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modelled simulation of the effect of various factors (including GHGs, Solar irradiance) singly and in combination, showing in particular that solar activity produces a small and nearly uniform warming, unlike what is observed.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Solar_sunspot_maximum" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar sunspot maximum">Solar sunspot maximum</a> occurs when the magnetic field of the sun collapses and reverse as part of its average 11 year <a href="/wiki/Solar_cycle" title="Solar cycle">solar cycle</a> (22 years for complete North to North restoration).
</p><p>The role of the sun in recent climate change has been looked at by climate scientists. Since 1978, <a href="/wiki/Solar_variation" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar variation">output from the Sun</a> has been measured by <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a><sup id="cite_ref-nrc2008_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrc2008-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>6</span></sup> significantly more accurately than was previously possible from the surface. These measurements indicate that the Sun's total <a href="/wiki/Solar_irradiance" title="Solar irradiance">solar irradiance</a> has not increased since 1978, so the warming during the past 30 years cannot be directly attributed to an increase in total solar energy reaching the Earth (see graph above, left). In the three decades since 1978, the combination of solar and <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_activity" class="mw-redirect" title="Volcanic activity">volcanic activity</a> probably had a slight cooling influence on the climate.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Climate models have been used to examine the role of the sun in recent climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
Models are unable to reproduce the rapid warming observed in recent decades when they only take into account variations in total solar irradiance and volcanic activity. Models are, however, able to simulate the observed 20th century changes in temperature when they include all of the most important external forcings, including human influences and natural forcings. As has already been stated, Hegerl <i>et al.</i> (2007) concluded that greenhouse gas forcing had "very likely" caused most of the observed global warming since the mid-20th century. In making this conclusion, Hegerl <i>et al.</i> (2007) allowed for the possibility that climate models had been underestimated the effect of solar forcing.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The role of solar activity in climate change has also been calculated over <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">longer time periods</a> using "proxy" datasets, such as <a href="/wiki/Tree_rings" class="mw-redirect" title="Tree rings">tree rings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
Models indicate that solar and volcanic forcings can explain periods of relative warmth and cold between <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">A.D.</a>&#160;1000 and 1900, but human-induced forcings are needed to reproduce the late-20th century warming.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another line of evidence against the sun having caused recent climate change comes from looking at how temperatures at different levels in the Earth's atmosphere have changed.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
Models and observations (see figure above, middle) show that greenhouse gas results in warming of the lower atmosphere at the surface (called the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>) but cooling of the upper atmosphere (called the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">Depletion</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> by chemical <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">refrigerants</a> has also resulted in a cooling effect in the stratosphere. If the sun was responsible for observed warming, warming of the troposphere at the surface and warming at the top of the stratosphere would be expected as increase solar activity would replenish ozone and oxides of nitrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The stratosphere has a reverse temperature gradient than the troposphere so as the temperature of the troposphere cools with altitude, the stratosphere rises with altitude. <a href="/wiki/Hadley_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Hadley cells">Hadley cells</a> are the mechanism by which equatorial generated ozone in the tropics (highest area of UV irradiance in the stratosphere) is moved poleward. Global climate models suggest that climate change may widen the Hadley cells and push the jetstream northward thereby expanding the tropics region and resulting in warmer, dryer conditions in those areas overall.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-consensus_views">Non-consensus views</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Radiative-forcings.svg" class="image"><img alt="Refer to caption" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/220px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/330px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Radiative-forcings.svg/440px-Radiative-forcings.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Radiative-forcings.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contribution of natural factors and human activities to <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a> of climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc_radiative_forcing_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_radiative_forcing-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Radiative forcing values are for the year 2005, relative to the pre-industrial era (1750).<sup id="cite_ref-ipcc_radiative_forcing_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_radiative_forcing-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The contribution of solar irradiance to radiative forcing is 5% the value of the combined radiative forcing due to increases in the atmospheric concentrations of carbon dioxide, methane and nitrous oxide.<sup id="cite_ref-epa_solar_activity_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa_solar_activity-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_denial" title="Climate change denial">Climate change denial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_atmospheres" class="mw-redirect" title="Extraterrestrial atmospheres">extraterrestrial atmospheres</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Habibullo_Abdussamatov" class="mw-redirect" title="Habibullo Abdussamatov">Habibullo Abdussamatov</a> (2004), head of space research at St. Petersburg's <a href="/wiki/Pulkovo_Observatory" title="Pulkovo Observatory">Pulkovo Astronomical Observatory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, has argued that the sun is responsible for recently observed climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Journalist" title="Journalist">Journalists</a> for <a href="/wiki/News" title="News">news</a> sources <a href="/wiki/Canada.com" class="mw-redirect" title="Canada.com">canada.com</a> (Solomon, 2007b),<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_News" class="mw-redirect" title="National Geographic News">National Geographic News</a> (Ravillious, 2007),<sup id="cite_ref-natgeo_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/LiveScience" class="mw-redirect" title="LiveScience">LiveScience</a> (Than, 2007)<sup id="cite_ref-than_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-than-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> reported on the story of warming on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>. In these articles, Abdussamatov was quoted. He stated that warming on Mars was evidence that global warming on Earth was being caused by changes in the sun.
</p><p>Ravillious (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-natgeo_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> quoted two scientists who disagreed with Abdussamatov: Amato Evan, a climate scientist at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin-Madison" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Wisconsin-Madison">University of Wisconsin-Madison</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">US</a>, and Colin Wilson, a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_science" title="Planetary science">planetary physicist</a> at <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a>. According to Wilson, "Wobbles in the orbit of Mars are the main cause of its climate change in the current era" (see also <a href="/wiki/Orbital_forcing" title="Orbital forcing">orbital forcing</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Than (2007) quoted Charles Long, a climate physicist at <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest_National_Laboratory" title="Pacific Northwest National Laboratory">Pacific Northwest National Laboratories</a> in the US, who disagreed with Abdussamatov.<sup id="cite_ref-than_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-than-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
Than (2007) pointed to the view of <a href="/wiki/Benny_Peiser" title="Benny Peiser">Benny Peiser</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Social_anthropologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Social anthropologist">social anthropologist</a> at <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_John_Moores_University" title="Liverpool John Moores University">Liverpool John Moores University</a> in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-than_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-than-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In his <a href="/wiki/Newsletter" title="Newsletter">newsletter</a>, Peiser had cited a <a href="/wiki/Blog" title="Blog">blog</a> that had commented on warming observed on several planetary bodies in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar system">Solar system</a>. These included <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>'s moon <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> and Mars. In an e-mail interview with Than (2007), Peiser stated that:</p><blockquote><p>"I think it is an intriguing coincidence that warming trends have been observed on a number of very diverse planetary bodies in our solar system, (...) Perhaps this is just a fluke."</p></blockquote><p>Than (2007) provided alternative explanations of why warming had occurred on Triton, Pluto, Jupiter and Mars.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/US_Environmental_Protection_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="US Environmental Protection Agency">US Environmental Protection Agency</a> (US EPA, 2009) responded to public comments on climate change attribution.<sup id="cite_ref-epa_solar_activity_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa_solar_activity-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> A number of commenters had argued that recent climate change could be attributed to changes in solar irradiance. According to the US EPA (2009), this attribution was not supported by the bulk of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_literature" title="Scientific literature">scientific literature</a>. Citing the work of the IPCC (2007), the US EPA pointed to the low contribution of solar irradiance to radiative forcing since the start of the Industrial Revolution in 1750. Over this time period (1750 to 2005),<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> the estimated contribution of solar irradiance to radiative forcing was 5% the value of the combined radiative forcing due to increases in the atmospheric concentrations of carbon dioxide, methane and nitrous oxide (see graph opposite).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_of_cosmic_rays">Effect of cosmic rays</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Henrik_Svensmark" title="Henrik Svensmark">Henrik Svensmark</a> has suggested that the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnetic">magnetic</a> activity of the sun deflects <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, and that this may influence the generation of <a href="/wiki/Cloud_condensation_nuclei" title="Cloud condensation nuclei">cloud condensation nuclei</a>, and thereby have an effect on the climate.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The website <a href="/wiki/ScienceDaily" class="mw-redirect" title="ScienceDaily">ScienceDaily</a> reported on a 2009 study that looked at how past changes in climate have been affected by the Earth's magnetic field.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Geophysicist Mads Faurschou Knudsen, who co-authored the study, stated that the study's results supported Svensmark's theory. The authors of the study also acknowledged that CO<sub>2</sub> plays an important role in climate change.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Consensus_view_on_cosmic_rays">Consensus view on cosmic rays</span></h4><p>
The view that cosmic rays could provide the mechanism by which changes in solar activity affect climate is not supported by the literature.<sup id="cite_ref-epa_cosmic_rays_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa_cosmic_rays-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Solomon <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> state:</p><blockquote><p>[..] the cosmic ray time series does not appear to correspond to global total cloud cover after 1991 or to global low-level cloud cover after 1994. Together with the lack of a proven physical mechanism and the plausibility of other causal factors affecting changes in cloud cover, this makes the association between galactic cosmic ray-induced changes in aerosol and cloud formation controversial</p></blockquote>
<p>Studies by Lockwood and Fröhlich (2007)<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> and Sloan and Wolfendale (2008)<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> found no relation between warming in recent decades and cosmic rays. Pierce and Adams (2009)<sup id="cite_ref-pierce_adams_2009_cosmic_rays_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pierce_adams_2009_cosmic_rays-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> used a model to simulate the effect of cosmic rays on cloud properties. They concluded that the hypothesized effect of cosmic rays was too small to explain recent climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-pierce_adams_2009_cosmic_rays_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pierce_adams_2009_cosmic_rays-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Pierce and Adams (2009)<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> noted that their findings did not rule out a possible connection between cosmic rays and climate change, and recommended further research.
</p><p>
Erlykin <i>et al.</i> (2009)<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> found that the evidence showed that connections between solar variation and climate were more likely to be mediated by direct variation of insolation rather than cosmic rays, and concluded: "Hence within our assumptions, the effect of varying solar activity, either by direct solar irradiance or by varying cosmic ray rates, must be less than 0.07 °C since 1956, i.e. less than 14% of the observed global warming." Carslaw (2009)<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> and Pittock (2009)<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> review the recent and historical literature in this field and continue to find that the link between cosmic rays and climate is tenuous, though they encourage continued research. US EPA (2009)<sup id="cite_ref-epa_cosmic_rays_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa_cosmic_rays-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> commented on research by Duplissy <i>et al.</i> (2009):<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>The CLOUD experiments at <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">CERN</a> are interesting research but do not provide conclusive evidence that cosmic rays can serve as a major source of cloud seeding. Preliminary results from the experiment (Duplissy et al., 2009) suggest that though there was some evidence of ion mediated nucleation, for most of the nucleation events observed the contribution of ion processes appeared to be minor. These experiments also showed the difficulty in maintaining sufficiently clean conditions and stable temperatures to prevent spurious aerosol bursts. There is no indication that the earlier Svensmark experiments could even have matched the controlled conditions of the CERN experiment. We find that the Svensmark results on cloud seeding have not yet been shown to be robust or sufficient to materially alter the conclusions of the assessment literature, especially given the abundance of recent literature that is skeptical of the cosmic ray-climate linkage</p></blockquote>

<li id="cite_note-ar4_uncertainty-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ar4_uncertainty_34-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ar4_uncertainty_34-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ar4_uncertainty_34-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ar4_uncertainty_34-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
In the IPCC Fourth Assessment Report, the following scale is used to quantify uncertainty: "virtually certain" &gt;99%; "extremely likely" &gt;95%; "very likely" &gt;90%; "likely" &gt;66%; "more likely than not" &gt;50%; "about as likely as not" 33 to 66%; "unlikely" &lt;33%; "very unlikely" &lt;10%; "extremely unlikely" &lt;5%; "exceptionally unlikely" &lt;1%. <cite id="CITEREFSolomon,_S." class="citation">Solomon, S.;  et al., <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc.ch/publications_and_data/ar4/wg1/en/tssts-2.html">"Box TS.1: Treatment of Uncertainties in the Working Group I Assessment"</a>, <i>Technical Summary</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Box+TS.1%3A+Treatment+of+Uncertainties+in+the+Working+Group+I+Assessment&amp;rft.btitle=Technical+Summary&amp;rft.au=Solomon%2C+S.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.ipcc.ch%2Fpublications_and_data%2Far4%2Fwg1%2Fen%2Ftssts-2.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAttribution+of+recent+climate+change" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/>, in <a href="#CITEREFIPCC_AR4_WG12007">IPCC AR4 WG1 2007</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-hegerl_2007_executive_summary-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-hegerl_2007_executive_summary_35-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-hegerl_2007_executive_summary_35-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Hegerl <i>et al.</i>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc.ch/publications_and_data/ar4/wg1/en/ch9.html">Chapter 9: Understanding and Attributing Climate Change</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc.ch/publications_and_data/ar4/wg1/en/ch9s9-es.html">Executive Summary</a>, in
<a href="#CITEREFIPCC_AR4_WG12007">IPCC AR4 WG1 2007</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-solomon_2007_drivers_of_climate_change-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-solomon_2007_drivers_of_climate_change_36-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-solomon_2007_drivers_of_climate_change_36-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
